<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact">Impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Degrassi:_The_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Television_ratings">Television ratings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Degrassi:_The_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Television ratings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With characters from <i>Degrassi Junior High</i> and <i>Degrassi High</i> appearing in <i>Degrassi: The Next Generation</i>, viewers of the earlier series who are in their 20s and 30s make up a dedicated fan base of the current incarnation. Approximately 40% of the series' viewers are outside of <i>Degrassi: The Next Generation</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s 13- to 17-year-old target audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nostalgia_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-nostalgia-107">[107]</a></sup> <i>Degrassi: The Next Generation</i> averaged 365,000 viewers aged 13–20 years old in season one, and became the most watched domestic drama in Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-calgary_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-calgary-108">[108]</a></sup> By the end of season two, it had become the most popular Canadian show for the three youngest age groups (children aged 2–12, teenagers aged 13–17 and young adults aged 18–34).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the third season, <i>Degrassi: The Next Generation</i> was again the most-watched all-Canadian drama series, and the most watched Canadian drama among adults 18–49.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> A season four episode that features a school shooting received 930,000 viewers; at that time it was the programme's highest-ever rating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CTV-NYT_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-CTV-NYT-111">[111]</a></sup> A second episode in the same season that features a storyline about <a href="/wiki/Oral_sex" title="Oral sex">oral sex</a> also earned just under 1,000,000 viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oral_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-oral-112">[112]</a></sup> Overall, the season averaged 600,000 viewers, and was again the top Canadian drama for teens aged 13–17, and adults in three age brackets 18–34, 18–49 and 25–54.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CTV-NYT_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-CTV-NYT-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oral_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-oral-112">[112]</a></sup> It averaged 250,000 viewers in the US in 2004 and was the highest rated digital cable series in the US in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fast_times_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-fast_times-113">[113]</a></sup> While that figure is still far lower than successful shows on the "big four" networks (<a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a>, <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">Fox</a> and <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a>), the premiere episodes of earlier seasons have achieved higher audience figures with females aged 13–34.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broadcasting_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Broadcasting-92">[92]</a></sup> 2004 also saw the school-shooting episode receive more than half a million US viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sabrina_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-sabrina-114">[114]</a></sup> The fifth season drew in an average of 767,000 viewers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-17">[17]</a></sup> with episode two of the season being watched by 1,000,000 viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-million_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-million-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the most recent seasons, however, the audience figures have dropped considerably. In Canada, season six was watched by fewer viewers than had watched season five;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-17">[17]</a></sup> episode fourteen was the highest-viewed episode of the season, with a total of 645,000 viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The season finale was watched by 520,000 viewers, and the season overall averaged 522,000 viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-17">[17]</a></sup> The average viewing figures fell again during the seventh season. The season premiere achieved the highest figures with 585,000 viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Myth_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Myth-117">[117]</a></sup> This progressively dropped over the coming weeks, from 446,000 total viewers for the third episode,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> to 407,000 total viewers for the fifth episode,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> and continued to fall to a low of 314,000 viewers by the tenth episode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mercer_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mercer-120">[120]</a></sup> Overall, the first twelve episodes of the season averaged 455,000 viewers, 45,000 less than the same number of episodes from the season six.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Viewing figures continued to fall throughout season eight; Bill Brioux, the television columnist for <a href="/wiki/The_Canadian_Press" title="The Canadian Press">The Canadian Press</a>, was surprised that <i>Degrassi: The Next Generation</i> had even reached its eighth season with such poor ratings, asking "What other show in the history of Canadian or American television has so consistently drawn so few viewers yet gets renewed year after year?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uptown_ratings_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Uptown_ratings-121">[121]</a></sup> The season premiere was watched by 398,000 viewers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uptown_ratings_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Uptown_ratings-121">[121]</a></sup> almost 200,000 viewers fewer than what the premiere of season seven achieved. Viewing figures continued to drop when episodes two and six were both watched by an average of 220,000 viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheels_off_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wheels_off-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> At the time they were the lowest figures <i>Degrassi: The Next Generation</i> has ever received;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheels_off_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wheels_off-122">[122]</a></sup> however, they continued to fall and by episode eleven, overnight ratings indicated it had received 139,000 viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>The overall number of viewers rose slightly for the thirteenth episode, the first of a two-parter, when it was watched by 157,000 people, but the viewing figures for the key 18–34 demographics was at a low of 81,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> The following week, the episode that concluded the two-parter picked up viewers, reaching an estimated total of 206,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jane_Says_P2_figures_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jane_Says_P2_figures-18">[18]</a></sup> Brioux commented again about <i>Degrassi: The Next Generation</i> still being on the schedules, wondering when CTV was going to announce its cancellation and noting that <i><a href="/wiki/The_Amazing_Race" title="The Amazing Race">The Amazing Race</a></i>, which follows it in the scheduling, was watched by ten times the number of <i>Degrassi</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jane_Says_P2_figures_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-Jane_Says_P2_figures-18">[18]</a></sup> That pattern was repeated the following week, when <i>Degrassi: The Next Generation</i> was watched by 222,000 viewers, compared to 1,834,000 viewers for <i>The Amazing Race</i>, 1,579,000 viewers for <i><a href="/wiki/Desperate_Housewives" title="Desperate Housewives">Desperate Housewives</a></i> and 1,106,000 viewers for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mentalist" title="The Mentalist">The Mentalist</a></i>, which were broadcast by CTV later in the evening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>CTV aired two episodes back-to-back in the first half of season nine, and the scheduling had improved ratings. The first two episodes earned a combined figure of 471,000 viewers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> and the third and fourth episodes retained them; they were watched by a combined 475,000 viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> The following week, the total viewing figures for episodes five and six had increased to 608,000,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> and remained high as the season went into <a href="/wiki/Hiatus_(television)" title="Hiatus (television)">hiatus</a> in November with 572,000 total viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards">Awards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Degrassi:_The_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Awards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_awards_and_nominations_received_by_the_Degrassi_franchise" title="List of awards and nominations received by the Degrassi franchise">List of awards and nominations received by the Degrassi franchise</a></div>
<p><i>Degrassi: The Next Generation</i> has won over fifty awards, and has been nominated for many others. The <a href="/wiki/Writers_Guild_of_Canada" title="Writers Guild of Canada">Writers Guild of Canada</a> has awarded its <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Screenwriting_Awards" title="Canadian Screenwriting Awards">Canadian Screenwriting Awards</a> to the writers of two episodes. In 2004, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aaron_Martin_(producer)" title="Aaron Martin (producer)">Aaron Martin</a>, James Hurst and Shelley Scarrow won the "Best Youth Script Award" for "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pride_(D:TNG_episode)" title="Pride (D:TNG episode)">Pride</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> The following year, the Scarrow-penned episode "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secret,_part_1_(D:TNG_episode)" title="Secret, part 1 (D:TNG episode)">Secret</a>" vied with "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercy_Street_(D:TNG_episode)" title="Mercy Street (D:TNG episode)">Mercy Street</a>", written by James Hurst and Miklos Perlus for the "Best Youth Script Award".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whats_new_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_new-132">[132]</a></sup> "Mercy Street" won.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> The series has been nominated for fourteen <a href="/wiki/Directors_Guild_of_Canada" title="Directors Guild of Canada">Directors Guild of Canada Awards</a>. In the "Outstanding Achievement in a Television Series – Children's" group category, the Bruce McDonald helmed "<a href="/wiki/Mother_and_Child_Reunion_(Degrassi:_The_Next_Generation)" title="Mother and Child Reunion (Degrassi: The Next Generation)">Mother and Child Reunion</a>" (nominated 2002) and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/When_Doves_Cry_(D:TNG_episode)" title="When Doves Cry (D:TNG episode)">When Doves Cry</a>" (nominated 2003) were winners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2002DGCA_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-2002DGCA-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2003DGCA_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-2003DGCA-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>"<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_Wedding_(D:TNG_episode)" title="White Wedding (D:TNG episode)">White Wedding</a>", also directed by McDonald, won the award in 2003 for "Outstanding Achievement in Direction – Television Series".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2003DGCA_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-2003DGCA-135">[135]</a></sup> McDonald's "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holiday_(D:TNG_episode)" title="Holiday (D:TNG episode)">Holiday</a>" (nominated 2004), and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Stefan_Scaini&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Stefan Scaini (page does not exist)">Stefan Scaini</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Time_Stands_Still_(Degrassi:_The_Next_Generation)" title="Time Stands Still (Degrassi: The Next Generation)">Time Stands Still, part 2</a>" (nominated 2005) won the group categories for "Outstanding Achievement in a Television Series – Family".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CTV_awards_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-CTV_awards-136">[136]</a></sup> "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Can%27t_Hardly_Wait_(D:TNG_episode)" title="Can't Hardly Wait (D:TNG episode)">Can't Hardly Wait</a>" and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pass_the_Dutchie_(D:TNG_episode)" title="Pass the Dutchie (D:TNG episode)">Pass the Dutchie</a>" were also nominated in that category in 2007 and 2008, respectively, but failed to win the awards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007DGCA_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-2007DGCA-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2008DGCA_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-2008DGCA-138">[138]</a></sup> Stephen Withrow has picked up two awards in the "Outstanding Achievement in Picture Editing" category, for "Mother and Child Reunion" in 2002 and "When Doves Cry" in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2002DGCA_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-2002DGCA-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2003DGCA_135-2"><a href="#cite_note-2003DGCA-135">[135]</a></sup> <i>Degrassi: The Next Generation</i> has won seventeen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gemini_Award" title="Gemini Award">Gemini Awards</a> since 2002, and has been nominated in twenty-six other categories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Degrassi_search_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Degrassi_search-139">[139]</a></sup> In 2010, producer <a href="/wiki/Linda_Schuyler" title="Linda Schuyler">Linda Schuyler</a> received the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Achievement_Award" title="Academy Achievement Award">Academy Achievement Award</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linda_Schuyler_and_Jordan_Todosey_at_the_70th_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2120" data-file-width="3184" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Linda_Schuyler_and_Jordan_Todosey_at_the_70th_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg/220px-Linda_Schuyler_and_Jordan_Todosey_at_the_70th_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Linda_Schuyler_and_Jordan_Todosey_at_the_70th_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg/330px-Linda_Schuyler_and_Jordan_Todosey_at_the_70th_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Linda_Schuyler_and_Jordan_Todosey_at_the_70th_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg/440px-Linda_Schuyler_and_Jordan_Todosey_at_the_70th_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linda_Schuyler_and_Jordan_Todosey_at_the_70th_Annual_Peabody_Awards.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Linda Schuyler and Jordan Todosey holding award at the 70th Annual Peabody Awards</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Degrassi: The Next Generation</i> has also seen awards success internationally. It was nominated for a "Best Children's Television Programme" Prix Jeunesse in Germany in 2004,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PJ2_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-PJ2-141">[141]</a></sup> and has been nominated at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GLAAD_Media_Awards" title="GLAAD Media Awards">GLAAD Media Awards</a> four times. In 2004, the show received a nomination in the Outstanding Drama Series category,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GLAAD_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-GLAAD-142">[142]</a></sup> but lost to the sports drama <i><a href="/wiki/Playmakers" title="Playmakers">Playmakers</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> It was nominated in the same category again in 2008, but lost to <i><a href="/wiki/Brothers_%26_Sisters_(2006_TV_series)" title="Brothers &amp; Sisters (2006 TV series)">Brothers &amp; Sisters</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GLAAD08_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-GLAAD08-145">[145]</a></sup> In 2005, <i>Degrassi: The Next Generation</i> won the <a href="/wiki/Television_Critics_Association" title="Television Critics Association">Television Critics Association Award</a> for "Outstanding Achievement in Children's Programming." It was only the second time that a non-United States series has won an award in this category (the first time was <i>Degrassi Junior High</i> in 1988).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Young_Artist_Awards" title="Young Artist Awards">Young Artist Awards</a> has been recognising actors in the Degrassi franchise since 1987. <i>Degrassi: The Next Generation</i> was nominated for four awards in its first year. Ryan Cooley and Jake Goldsbie were nominated in the "Best Leading Young Actor Performance in a TV Comedy Series" category, but lost to <a href="/wiki/Frankie_Muniz" title="Frankie Muniz">Frankie Muniz</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/Malcolm_in_the_Middle" title="Malcolm in the Middle">Malcolm in the Middle</a></i>. The series won the award for "Best Ensemble in a Comedy or Drama TV Series" category.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> A year later, Jake Epstein won the Young Artist Award in the category for "Best Leading Young Actor Performance in a TV Comedy Series".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24YAA_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-24YAA-148">[148]</a></sup> In 2005, Christina Schmidt tied with <a href="/wiki/Alia_Shawkat" title="Alia Shawkat">Alia Shawkat</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Arrested_Development_(TV_series)" title="Arrested Development (TV series)">Arrested Development</a></i> to win the award for "Best Supporting Young Actress Performance in a TV Comedy Series",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26YAA_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-26YAA-149">[149]</a></sup> and Jamie Johnston won the 2008 category for "Best Leading Young Actor Performance in a TV Series".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29YAA_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-29YAA-150">[150]</a></sup> Young Artist Awards were awarded again in 2012, with both <a href="/wiki/Cristine_Prosperi" title="Cristine Prosperi">Cristine Prosperi</a> and <a href="/wiki/A.J._Saudin" title="A.J. Saudin">A.J. Saudin</a> winning awards in the Lead Young Actress and Recurring Young Actor categories respectively. However, they both tied with another in their category.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teen_Choice_Award" title="Teen Choice Award">Teen Choice Awards</a>, children aged between twelve and nineteen vote for each category's winner. The series has been nominated three times in the "Choice Summer TV Show" category, and has won twice, in 2005 and 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> The episode "<a href="/wiki/My_Body_Is_a_Cage" title="My Body Is a Cage">My Body Is a Cage</a>", where Adam's transgender secret is revealed, earned a <a href="/wiki/Peabody_Award" title="Peabody Award">Peabody Award</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Creative_Arts_Emmy_Award" title="Creative Arts Emmy Award">Creative Arts Emmy Award</a> nomination<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> in 2011.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_reception">Critical reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Degrassi:_The_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Critical reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Degrassi: The Next Generation</i> has received generally positive reviews. <i><a href="/wiki/Entertainment_Weekly" title="Entertainment Weekly">Entertainment Weekly</a></i> has called it "a cult hit", and <i>The New York Times</i> named it "Tha Best Teen TV N da WRLD (The best teen TV in the world)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fast_times_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-fast_times-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-156">[156]</a></sup> Of the first season, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Ottawa_Citizen" title="The Ottawa Citizen">The Ottawa Citizen</a>'</i>s Tony Atherton had mixed feelings of the new incarnation, saying it "has a cleaner, more polished look, has lost its edge [and offers] nothing new to viewers familiar with the groundbreaking <a href="/wiki/Degrassi_High" title="Degrassi High">preceding series</a>, nor to anyone else who has watched the deluge of teen dramas since ... there is a sense of déjà vu with regards to the plots and characters".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atherton_review_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atherton_review-157">[157]</a></sup> He did, however, praise the show for having "the same simple narrative told from a kid's viewpoint, and the same regard for unvarnished reality [as <i>Degrassi Junior High</i> and <i>Degrassi High</i>]".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atherton_review_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-Atherton_review-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>Before its debut in the United States, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Seattle_Times" title="The Seattle Times">The Seattle Times</a></i>' Melanie McFarland wondered whether the series would do well, writing: "soft-pedaling through the issues might work for today's family of viewers, but what's gentle enough for Mom and Dad's peace of mind might not be enough to hook Junior or the original Degrassi's older fans".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> The issues that the characters experience have often been commented on in the media. It has been noted that the series never attempts to hide from depicting honest accounts of the trials and tribulations that real teenagers may often experience. Sarah Liss from <a href="/wiki/CBC_News" title="CBC News">CBC News</a> said that despite often being corny and soap opera-y, <i>Degrassi: The Next Generation</i> tackles issues that other genre series prefer to gloss over, and was part of her essential viewing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liss_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Liss-159">[159]</a></sup> She named the series one of "the [ten] most important television shows of the 2000s", and was the only children's series, and the only Canadian television series, to appear on the list, which included <i><a href="/wiki/Mad_Men" title="Mad Men">Mad Men</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Lost_(TV_series)" title="Lost (TV series)">Lost</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/CSI_(franchise)" title="CSI (franchise)">CSI franchise</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Sex_and_the_City" title="Sex and the City">Sex and the City</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liss_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-Liss-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2008, Jeffrey Bento-Carrier described one storyline that showed a teacher being accused of sexually assaulting one of his students as "shock[ing]", adding that "Degrassi is not for everyone, mainly because it's an honest account what it's like to be a teen in a society which values cliques and confrontation over truth and real growth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bento_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bento-160">[160]</a></sup> Brian Orloff of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Petersburg_Times" title="St. Petersburg Times">St. Petersburg Times</a></i> echoed the sentiments, and praised the series for "stay[ing] in touch with teens' lives".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>In spite of these comments, The N held back one of the more controversial episodes of the first season, which showed a character losing control after taking an ecstasy pill, and refused to broadcast it until it was presented with an edited copy from the producers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jagged_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jagged-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> The N also refused to broadcast two episodes from the second season that featured a storyline about date-rape until suitable edits could be made,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-outcry_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-outcry-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-delayed_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-delayed-165">[165]</a></sup> and withheld other episodes from season three that showed a fourteen-year-old character having an <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a> after having consensual <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">sexual intercourse</a> with her boyfriend, and feeling no regrets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-interim_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-interim-166">[166]</a></sup> The decision caused an uproar amongst fans who organized a petition that caught the attention of the <i>New York Times</i>, as well as CBC, the <i><a href="/wiki/National_Post" title="National Post">National Post</a></i> and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/London_Free_Press" title="London Free Press">London Free Press</a></i> in Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-outcry_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-outcry-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-delayed_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-delayed-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> The episodes eventually aired three years later as part of an "every episode ever" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marathon_(television)" title="Marathon (television)">marathon</a>, with very little advertisement from the network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> Another storyline was featured in the media after ten children from a <a href="/wiki/French-speaking_Quebecer" title="French-speaking Quebecer">Québécois</a> school were found to have a number of cuts on their bodies. They said they had copied the show when one character began <a href="/wiki/Cutting" title="Cutting">cutting</a> herself in an episode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>Comparisons between <i>Degrassi: The Next Generation</i> and other genre specific series have also been made throughout the run. Jake Surette, a writer with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AfterElton.com" title="AfterElton.com">AfterElton.com</a>, a website which focuses on the portrayal of homosexual and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bisexual" title="Bisexual">bisexual</a> men in the media, reported on the portrayal of two <i>Degrassi: The Next Generation</i> gay characters. "<i>Degrassi</i> features ongoing stories of real-life teen dilemmas—including intense gay and lesbian storylines—and does it without the righteous, 'On a Very Special <i><a href="/wiki/Blossom_(TV_series)" title="Blossom (TV series)">Blossom</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span> endings that many teen dramas and sitcoms thrive on."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elton_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elton-170">[170]</a></sup> Kevin Thompson of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Palm_Beach_Post" title="The Palm Beach Post">The Palm Beach Post</a></i> said the series "is told from a teenager's point of view since the writers have no interest in appealing to a broad-based demographic like the writers on, say, Fox's <i><a href="/wiki/The_O.C." title="The O.C.">The O.C.</a></i> ... it connects with teens on their level".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/PopMatters" title="PopMatters">PopMatters</a>'s Jodie Janella Horn also compared it with <i>The O.C.</i>, saying that while scenes from <i>Degrassi</i> could be "actual scenes from my actual teenage life ... <i>The O.C.</i> will never remind me of anything in my life", adding that it is the most unnervingly accurate series ever of the high school genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/San_Jose_Mercury_News" title="San Jose Mercury News">San Jose Mercury News</a></i> has said "If they [<i><a href="/wiki/Everwood" title="Everwood">Everwood</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_O.C." title="The O.C.">The O.C.</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/One_Tree_Hill_(TV_series)" title="One Tree Hill (TV series)">One Tree Hill</a></i>] want to be taken seriously, the shows could take a cue from Canadian drama <i>Degrassi: The Next Generation,</i> which ... addresses the same gritty teen issues without being far-fetched",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> the <i>New York Times</i> has also made favourable reviews of the series in comparison to <i>Everwood</i>, <i>The O.C.</i>, and <i>One Tree Hill</i>, as well as <i><a href="/wiki/Beverly_Hills,_90210" title="Beverly Hills, 90210">Beverly Hills, 90210</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Gilmore_Girls" title="Gilmore Girls">Gilmore Girls</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Dawson%27s_Creek" title="Dawson's Creek">Dawson's Creek</a></i>, and adult series such as <i><a href="/wiki/Sex_and_the_City" title="Sex and the City">Sex and the City</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Maude_(TV_series)" title="Maude (TV series)">Maude</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Six_Feet_Under_(TV_series)" title="Six Feet Under (TV series)">Six Feet Under</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/AOL_TV" title="AOL TV">AOL TV</a> ranked it as the sixth TV's Biggest Guilty Pleasure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Degrassi:_The_Next_Generation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-p12_13-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-p12_13_13-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-p12_13_13-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFEllis2005">Ellis 2005</a>, pp. 12–13</span></li>
